algorithms.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202119-102) However, straightforward implementations, like those attempted by McCarthy and his students in the late 1960s, were especially intractable: the programs required astronomical numbers of steps to prove simple theorems.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200451-193)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993190%E2%80%93192-194) A more fruitful approach to logic was developed in the 1970s by [Robert Kowalski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Kowalski "Robert Kowalski") at the [University of Edinburgh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh "University of Edinburgh"), and soon this led to the collaboration with French researchers [Alain Colmerauer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alain_Colmerauer "Alain Colmerauer") and [Philippe Roussel](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Philippe_Roussel&action=edit&redlink=1 "Philippe Roussel (page does not exist)")[[fr](https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippe_Roussel "fr:Philippe Roussel")] who created the successful logic programming language [Prolog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prolog "Prolog").[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993193%E2%80%93196-195) Prolog uses a subset of logic ([Horn clauses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horn_clause "Horn clause"), closely related to "[rules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rule-based_system "Rule-based system")" and "[production rules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Production_system_(computer_science) "Production system (computer science)")") that permits tractable computation. Rules would continue to be influential, providing a foundation for [Edward Feigenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum "Edward Feigenbaum")'s [expert systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_systems "Expert systems") and the continuing work by [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Herbert A. Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon") that would lead to [Soar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soar_(cognitive_architecture) "Soar (cognitive architecture)") and their [unified theories of cognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unified_theories_of_cognition "Unified theories of cognition").[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993145%E2%80%93149,_258%E2%80%9363-196)

Critics of the logical approach noted, as [Dreyfus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus "Hubert Dreyfus") had, that human beings rarely used logic when they solved problems. Experiments by psychologists like [Peter Wason](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Cathcart_Wason "Peter Cathcart Wason"), [Eleanor Rosch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleanor_Rosch "Eleanor Rosch"), [Amos Tversky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amos_Tversky "Amos Tversky"), [Daniel Kahneman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Kahneman "Daniel Kahneman") and others provided proof.[[z]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-197) McCarthy responded that what people do is irrelevant. He argued that what is really needed are machines that can solve problems—not machines that think as people do.[[aa]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-198)

### MIT's "anti-logic" approach

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: MIT's \"anti-logic\" approach")]

Among the critics of [McCarthy's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") approach were his colleagues across the country at [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT"). [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky"), [Seymour Papert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seymour_Papert "Seymour Papert") and [Roger Schank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Schank "Roger Schank") were trying to solve problems like "story understanding" and "object recognition" that _required_ a machine to think like a person. To use ordinary concepts like "chair" or "restaurant" they had to make all the same illogical assumptions that people normally made. Unfortunately, imprecise concepts like these are hard to represent in logic. MIT chose instead to focus on writing programs that solved a given task without using high-level abstract definitions or general theories of cognition, and measured performance by iterative testing, rather than arguments from first principles. [Schank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Schank "Roger Schank") described their "anti-logic" approaches as [_scruffy_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies "Neats vs. scruffies"), as opposed to the [_neat_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies "Neats vs. scruffies") paradigm used by [McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)"), [Kowalski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Kowalski "Robert Kowalski"), [Feigenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum "Edward Feigenbaum"), [Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon").[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-199)[[ab]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-200)

In 1975, in a seminal paper, [Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") noted that many of his fellow researchers were using the same kind of tool: a framework that captures all our [common sense assumptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge "Commonsense knowledge") about something. For example, if we use the concept of a bird, there is a constellation of facts that immediately come to mind: we might assume that it flies, eats worms and so on (none of which are true for all birds). Minsky associated these assumptions with the general category and they could be _[inherited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inheritance\_(object-oriented\_programming) "Inheritance (object-oriented programming)")_ by the frames for subcategories and individuals, or over-ridden as necessary. He called these structures _[frames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frame\_(Artificial\_intelligence) "Frame (Artificial intelligence)")_. [Schank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Schank "Roger Schank") used a version of frames he called "[scripts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scripts_(artificial_intelligence) "Scripts (artificial intelligence)")" to successfully answer questions about short stories in English.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-201) Frames would eventually be widely used in [software engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_engineering "Software engineering") under the name [object-oriented programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming").

The logicians rose to the challenge. [Pat Hayes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pat_Hayes "Pat Hayes") claimed that "most of 'frames' is just a new syntax for parts of first-order logic." But he noted that "there are one or two apparently minor details which give a lot of trouble, however, especially defaults".[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes1981-202)

[Ray Reiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_Reiter "Raymond Reiter") admitted that "conventional logics, such as first-order logic, lack the expressive power to adequately represent the knowledge required for reasoning by default".[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReiter1978-203) He proposed augmenting first-order logic with a [closed world assumption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed_world_assumption "Closed world assumption") that a conclusion holds (by default) if its contrary cannot be shown. He showed how such an assumption corresponds to the common-sense assumption made in reasoning with frames. He also showed that it has its "procedural equivalent" as [negation as failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negation_as_failure "Negation as failure") in [Prolog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prolog "Prolog"). The closed world assumption, as formulated by Reiter, "is not a first-order notion. (It is a meta notion.)"[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReiter1978-203) However, [Keith Clark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keith_Clark_(computer_scientist) "Keith Clark (computer scientist)") showed that negation as _finite failure_ can be understood as reasoning implicitly with definitions in first-order logic including a [unique name assumption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unique_name_assumption "Unique name assumption") that different terms denote different individuals.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark1977-204)

During the late 1970s and throughout the 1980s, a variety of logics and extensions of first-order logic were developed both for negation as failure in [logic programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic_programming "Logic programming") and for default reasoning more generally. Collectively, these logics have become known as [non-monotonic logics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic "Non-monotonic logic").

In the 1980s, a form of AI program called "[expert systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_system "Expert system")" was adopted by corporations around the world and [knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_representation "Knowledge representation") became the focus of mainstream AI research. Governments provided substantial funding, such as Japan's [fifth generation computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fifth_generation_computer "Fifth generation computer") project and the U.S. [Strategic Computing Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Computing_Initiative "Strategic Computing Initiative"). "Overall, the AI industry boomed from a few million dollars in 1980 to billions of dollars in 1988."[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128)

### Expert systems become widely used

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Expert systems become widely used")]

An [expert system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_system "Expert system") is a program that answers questions or solves problems about a specific domain of knowledge, using logical [rules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Production_system_(computer_science) "Production system (computer science)") that are derived from the knowledge of experts.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-205) The earliest examples were developed by [Edward Feigenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Feigenbaum "Edward Feigenbaum") and his students. [Dendral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dendral "Dendral"), begun in 1965, identified compounds from spectrometer readings.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004327%E2%80%93335-206)[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202122-126)[MYCIN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MYCIN "MYCIN"), developed in 1972, diagnosed infectious blood diseases.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128) They demonstrated the feasibility of the approach.

Expert systems restricted themselves to a small domain of specific knowledge (thus avoiding the [commonsense knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge "Commonsense knowledge") problem)[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202122-126) and their simple design made it relatively easy for programs to be built and then modified once they were in place. All in all, the programs proved to be _useful_: something that AI had not been able to achieve up to this point.[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993158%E2%80%93159-207)

In 1980, an expert system called [R1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R1_(expert_system) "R1 (expert system)") was completed at [CMU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University "Carnegie Mellon University") for the [Digital Equipment Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation "Digital Equipment Corporation"). It was an enormous success: it was saving the company 40 million dollars annually by 1986.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993198-208) Corporations around the world began to develop and deploy expert systems and by 1985, they were spending over a billion dollars on AI, most of it in in-house AI departments.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994259-209) An industry grew up to support them, including hardware companies like [Symbolics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolics "Symbolics") and [Lisp Machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lisp_Machines "Lisp Machines") and software companies such as [IntelliCorp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IntelliCorp_(Software) "IntelliCorp (Software)") and [Aion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cleverpath_AION_Business_Rules_Expert "Cleverpath AION Business Rules Expert").[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-210)

### Government funding increases

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Government funding increases")]

In 1981, the [Japanese Ministry of International Trade and Industry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_International_Trade_and_Industry "Ministry of International Trade and Industry") set aside $850 million for the [Fifth generation computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fifth_generation_computer "Fifth generation computer") project. Their objectives were to write programs and build machines that could carry on conversations, translate languages, interpret pictures, and reason like human beings.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-211) Much to the chagrin of [scruffies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neats_vs._scruffies "Neats vs. scruffies"), they initially chose [Prolog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prolog "Prolog") as the primary computer language for the project.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993195-212)

Other countries responded with new programs of their own. The UK began the £350 million [Alvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alvey "Alvey") project.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202123-213) A consortium of American companies formed the [Microelectronics and Computer Technology Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microelectronics_and_Computer_Technology_Corporation "Microelectronics and Computer Technology Corporation") (or "MCC") to fund large-scale projects in AI and information technology.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993240-214)[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202123-213)[DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") responded as well, founding the [Strategic Computing Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Computing_Initiative "Strategic Computing Initiative") and tripling its investment in AI between 1984 and 1988.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004426%E2%80%93432-215)[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENRC1999under_%22Shift_to_Applied_Research_Increases_Investment%22-216)

### Knowledge revolution

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Knowledge revolution")]

The power of expert systems came from the expert knowledge they contained. They were part of a new direction in AI research that had been gaining ground throughout the 70s. "AI researchers were beginning to suspect—reluctantly, for it violated the scientific canon of [parsimony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principle_of_parsimony "Principle of parsimony")—that intelligence might very well be based on the ability to use large amounts of diverse knowledge in different ways,"[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004299-217) writes [Pamela McCorduck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck "Pamela McCorduck"). "[T]he great lesson from the 1970s was that intelligent behavior depended very much on dealing with knowledge, sometimes quite detailed knowledge, of a domain where a given task lay".[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004421-218)[Knowledge based systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_based_system "Knowledge based system") and [knowledge engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_engineering "Knowledge engineering") became a major focus of AI research in the 1980s.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-219) It was hoped that vast databases would solve the [commonsense knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge "Commonsense knowledge") problem and provide the support that [commonsense reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning") required.

In the 1980s, some researchers attempted to attack the [commonsense knowledge problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning") directly, by creating a massive database that would contain all the mundane facts that the average person knows. [Douglas Lenat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Lenat "Douglas Lenat"), who started a database called [Cyc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyc "Cyc"), argued that there is no shortcut―the only way for machines to know the meaning of human concepts is to teach them, one concept at a time, by hand.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-220)

New directions in the 1980s
---------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: New directions in the 1980s")]

Although symbolic [knowledge representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_representation_and_reasoning "Knowledge representation and reasoning") and [logical reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_reasoning "Logical reasoning") produced useful applications in the 80s and received massive amounts of funding, it was still unable to solve problems in [perception](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perception "Perception"), [robotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robotics "Robotics"), [learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning") and [common sense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_sense_reasoning "Common sense reasoning"). A small number of scientists and engineers began to doubt that the symbolic approach would ever be sufficient for these tasks and developed other approaches, such as "[connectionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connectionism "Connectionism")", ["soft" computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_computing "Soft computing") and [reinforcement learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reinforcement_learning "Reinforcement learning"). [Nils Nilsson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson "Nils John Nilsson") called these approaches "sub-symbolic".

### Revival of neural networks: "connectionism"

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Revival of neural networks: \"connectionism\"")]

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hopfield-net-vector.svg)

A Hopfield net with four nodes

In 1982, physicist [John Hopfield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Hopfield "John Hopfield") was able to prove that a form of neural network (now called a "[Hopfield net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hopfield_net "Hopfield net")") could learn and process information, and provably converges after enough time under any fixed condition. It was a breakthrough, as it was previously thought that nonlinear networks would, in general, evolve chaotically.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESejnowski2018-221)[Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton") proved a similar result about a device called a "[Boltzmann machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boltzmann_machine "Boltzmann machine")".[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylorMetzMiller2024-222) (Hopfield and Hinton would eventually receive the [2024 Nobel prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physics "List of Nobel laureates in Physics") for this work.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylorMetzMiller2024-222)) In 1986, Hinton and [David Rumelhart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Rumelhart "David Rumelhart") popularized a method for training neural networks called "[backpropagation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backpropagation "Backpropagation")".[[ac]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-223) These three developments helped to revive the exploration of [artificial neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_network "Artificial neural network").[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993214%E2%80%93215-224)

Neural networks, along with several other similar models, received widespread attention after the 1986 publication of the _[Parallel Distributed Processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallel\_Distributed\_Processing "Parallel Distributed Processing")_, a two-volume collection of papers edited by [Rumelhart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Rumelhart "David Rumelhart") and psychologist [James McClelland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_McClelland_(psychologist) "James McClelland (psychologist)"). The new field was christened "[connectionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connectionism "Connectionism")" and there was a considerable debate between advocates of [symbolic AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_AI "Symbolic AI") and the "connectionists".[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128) Hinton called symbols the "[luminous aether](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_aether "Luminous aether") of AI"―that is, an unworkable and misleading model of intelligence.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128) This was a direct attack on the principles that inspired the [cognitive revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_revolution "Cognitive revolution").

Neural networks started to advance the state of the art in some specialist areas such as protein structure prediction. Following pioneering work from Terry Sejnowski,[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Qian1988-225) cascading multilayer perceptrons such as PhD[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Rost1993-226) and PsiPred[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-McGuffin2000-227) reached near-theoretical maximum accuracy in predicting secondary structure.

In 1990, [Yann LeCun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yann_LeCun "Yann LeCun") at [Bell Labs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_Labs "Bell Labs") used [convolutional neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convolutional_neural_networks "Convolutional neural networks") to recognize handwritten digits. The system was used widely in the 90s, reading zip codes and personal checks. This was the first genuinely useful application of neural networks.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126-228)[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202021%E2%80%9322-229)

### Robotics and embodied reason

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Robotics and embodied reason")]

[Rodney Brooks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rodney_Brooks "Rodney Brooks"), [Hans Moravec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Moravec "Hans Moravec") and others argued that, to show real intelligence, a machine needs to have a _body_—it needs to perceive, move, survive, and deal with the world.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004454%E2%80%93462-230) Sensorimotor skills are essential to higher level skills such as [commonsense reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning"). They can't be efficiently implemented using abstract symbolic reasoning, so AI should solve the problems of perception, mobility, manipulation and survival without using symbolic representation at all. These robotics researchers advocated building intelligence "from the bottom up".[[ad]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-232)

A precursor to this idea was [David Marr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Marr_(neuroscientist) "David Marr (neuroscientist)"), who had come to [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT") in the late 1970s from a successful background in theoretical neuroscience to lead the group studying [vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_vision "Computer vision"). He rejected all symbolic approaches (_both_[McCarthy's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") logic and [Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky")'s frames), arguing that AI needed to understand the physical machinery of vision from the bottom up before any symbolic processing took place. (Marr's work would be cut short by leukemia in 1980.)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993183%E2%80%93190-233)

In his 1990 paper "Elephants Don't Play Chess",[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990-234) robotics researcher Brooks took direct aim at the [physical symbol system hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_symbol_system "Physical symbol system"), arguing that symbols are not always necessary since "the world is its own best model. It is always exactly up to date. It always has every detail there is to be known. The trick is to sense it appropriately and often enough."[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks19903-235)

In the 1980s and 1990s, many [cognitive scientists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_science "Cognitive science") also rejected the symbol processing model of the mind and argued that the body was essential for reasoning, a theory called the "[embodied mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embodied_mind "Embodied mind") thesis".[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-236)

### Soft computing and probabilistic reasoning

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Soft computing and probabilistic reasoning")]

[Soft computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_computing "Soft computing") uses methods that work with incomplete and imprecise information. They do not attempt to give precise, logical answers, but give results that are only "probably" correct. This allowed them to solve problems that precise symbolic methods could not handle. Press accounts often claimed these tools could "think like a human".[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPollack1984-237)[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPollack1989-238)

[Judea Pearl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judea_Pearl "Judea Pearl")'s _Probabilistic Reasoning in Intelligent Systems: Networks of Plausible Inference_, an influential 1988 book[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPearl1988-239) brought [probability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability "Probability") and [decision theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decision_theory "Decision theory") into AI.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202125-240)[Fuzzy logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fuzzy_logic "Fuzzy logic"), developed by [Lofti Zadeh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lofti_Zadeh "Lofti Zadeh") in the 60s, began to be more widely used in AI and robotics. [Evolutionary computation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_computation "Evolutionary computation") and [artificial neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_networks "Artificial neural networks") also handle imprecise information, and are classified as "soft". In the 90s and early 2000s many other soft computing tools were developed and put into use, including [Bayesian networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayesian_networks "Bayesian networks"),[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202125-240)[hidden Markov models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hidden_Markov_models "Hidden Markov models"),[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202125-240)[information theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory "Information theory"), and [stochastic modeling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_modeling "Stochastic modeling"). These tools, in turn depended on advanced mathematical techniques such as classical [optimization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optimization_(mathematics) "Optimization (mathematics)"). For a time in the 1990s and early 2000s, these soft tools were studied by a subfield of AI called "[computational intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_intelligence "Computational intelligence")".[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPooleMackworthGoebel1998-241)

### Reinforcement learning

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Reinforcement learning")]

[Reinforcement learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reinforcement_learning "Reinforcement learning")[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021Section_23-242) rewards an agent every time it performs a desired action well, and may give negative rewards (or "punishments") when it performs poorly. It was described in the first half of the twentieth century by psychologists using animal models, such as [Thorndike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Thorndike "Edward Thorndike"),[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020120%E2%80%93124-243)[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021819-244)[Pavlov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivan_Pavlov "Ivan Pavlov")[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020124-245) and [Skinner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B.F._Skinner "B.F. Skinner").[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020152%E2%80%93156-246) In the 1950s, [Alan Turing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Turing "Alan Turing")[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021819-244)[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020125-247) and [Arthur Samuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Samuel_(computer_scientist) "Arthur Samuel (computer scientist)")[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021819-244) foresaw the role of reinforcement learning in AI.

A successful and influential research program was led by [Richard Sutton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_S._Sutton "Richard S. Sutton") and [Andrew Barto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Barto "Andrew Barto") beginning in 1972. Their collaboration revolutionized the study of reinforcement learning and decision making over the past four decades.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020127%E2%80%93129-248)[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202125,_820-249) In 1988, Sutton described machine learning in terms of [decision theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decision_theory "Decision theory") (i.e., the [Markov decision process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Markov_decision_process "Markov decision process")). This gave the subject a solid theoretical foundation and access to a large body of theoretical results developed in the field of [operations research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operations_research "Operations research").[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202125,_820-249)

Also in 1988, Sutton and Barto developed the "[temporal difference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporal_difference_learning "Temporal difference learning")" (TD) learning algorithm, where the agent is rewarded only when its _predictions_ show improvement. It significantly outperformed previous algorithms.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020140-250) TD-learning was used by Gerald Tesauro in 1992 in the program [TD-Gammon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TD-Gammon "TD-Gammon"), which played backgammon as well as the best human players. The program learned the game by playing against itself with zero prior knowledge.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020141-251) In an interesting case of interdisciplinary convergence, neurologists discovered in 1997 that the [dopamine reward system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reward_system "Reward system") in brains also uses a version of the TD-learning algorithm.[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian2020?-252)[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021820-253)[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchultzDayanMontague1997-254) TD learning would be become highly influential in the 21st century, used in both [AlphaGo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlphaGo "AlphaGo") and [AlphaZero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlphaZero "AlphaZero").[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021822-255)

Second AI winter (1990s)
------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Second AI winter (1990s)")]

The business community's fascination with AI rose and fell in the 1980s in the classic pattern of an [economic bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_bubble "Economic bubble"). As dozens of companies failed, the perception in the business world was that the technology was not viable.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994501,_511-256) The damage to AI's reputation would last into the 21st century. Inside the field, there was little agreement on the reasons for AI's failure to fulfill the dream of human-level intelligence that had captured the imagination of the world in the 1960s. Together, all these factors helped to fragment AI into competing subfields focused on particular problems or approaches, sometimes even under new names that disguised the tarnished pedigree of "artificial intelligence".[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004424-257)

Over the next 20 years, AI consistently delivered working solutions to specific isolated problems. By the late 1990s, it was being used throughout the technology industry, although somewhat behind the scenes. The success was due to [increasing computer power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_law "Moore's law"), by collaboration with other fields (such as [mathematical optimization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_optimization "Mathematical optimization") and [statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics "Statistics")) and using higher standards of scientific accountability.

The term "[AI winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_winter "AI winter")" was coined by researchers who had survived the funding cuts of 1974 when they became concerned that enthusiasm for expert systems had spiraled out of control and that disappointment would certainly follow.[[ae]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-259) Their fears were well founded: in the late 1980s and early 1990s, AI suffered a series of financial setbacks.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128)

The first indication of a change in weather was the sudden collapse of the market for specialized AI hardware in 1987. Desktop computers from [Apple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Computer "Apple Computer") and [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM") had been steadily gaining speed and power and in 1987, they became more powerful than the more expensive [Lisp machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lisp_machines "Lisp machines") made by [Symbolics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolics "Symbolics") and others. There was no longer a good reason to buy them. An entire industry worth half a billion dollars was demolished overnight.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-260)

Eventually, the earliest successful expert systems, such as [XCON](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/XCON "XCON"), proved too expensive to maintain. They were difficult to update, they could not learn, and they were "[brittle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittle_(software) "Brittle (software)")" (i.e., they could make grotesque mistakes when given unusual inputs). Expert systems proved useful, but only in a few special contexts.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-261)

In the late 1980s, the [Strategic Computing Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Computing_Initiative "Strategic Computing Initiative") cut funding to AI "deeply and brutally". New leadership at [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") had decided that AI was not "the next wave" and directed funds towards projects that seemed more likely to produce immediate results.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004430%E2%80%93431-262)

By 1991, the impressive list of goals penned in 1981 for Japan's [Fifth Generation Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fifth_generation_computer "Fifth generation computer") had not been met. Some of them, like "carry on a casual conversation", would not be accomplished for another 30 years. As with other AI projects, expectations had run much higher than what was actually possible.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-263)[[af]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-265)

Over 300 AI companies had shut down, gone bankrupt, or been acquired by the end of 1993, effectively ending the first commercial wave of AI.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994440-266) In 1994, [HP Newquist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HP_Newquist "HP Newquist") stated in _The Brain Makers_ that "The immediate future of artificial intelligence—in its commercial form—seems to rest in part on the continued success of neural networks."[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994440-266)

### AI behind the scenes

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: AI behind the scenes")]

In the 1990s, algorithms originally developed by AI researchers began to appear as parts of larger systems. AI had solved a lot of very difficult problems[[ag]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-267) and their solutions proved to be useful throughout the technology industry,[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENRC1999Artificial_Intelligence_in_the_90s-268)[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurzweil2005264-269) such as [data mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_mining "Data mining"), [industrial robotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_robots "Industrial robots"), logistics, [speech recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speech_recognition "Speech recognition"),[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Economist2007-270) banking software,[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECNN2006-271) medical diagnosis,[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECNN2006-271) and [Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google "Google")'s search engine.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlsen2004-272)[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlsen2006-273)

The field of AI received little or no credit for these successes in the 1990s and early 2000s. Many of AI's greatest innovations have been reduced to the status of just another item in the tool chest of computer science.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-274)[Nick Bostrom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom "Nick Bostrom") explains: "A lot of cutting-edge AI has filtered into general applications, often without being called AI because once something becomes useful enough and common enough it's not labeled AI anymore."[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECNN2006-271)

Many researchers in AI in the 1990s deliberately called their work by other names, such as [informatics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Informatics_(academic_field) "Informatics (academic field)"), [knowledge-based systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge-based_systems "Knowledge-based systems"), "cognitive systems" or [computational intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_intelligence "Computational intelligence"). In part, this may have been because they considered their field to be fundamentally different from AI, but also the new names helped to procure funding.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Economist2007-270)[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTETascarella2006-275)[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994532-276) In the commercial world at least, the failed promises of the AI winter continued to haunt AI research into the 2000s, as the _New York Times_ reported in 2005: "Computer scientists and software engineers avoided the term artificial intelligence for fear of being viewed as wild-eyed dreamers."[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkoff2005-277)

### Mathematical rigor, greater collaboration, and a narrow focus

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Mathematical rigor, greater collaboration, and a narrow focus")]

AI researchers began to develop and use sophisticated mathematical tools more than they ever had in the past.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004486%E2%80%93487-278)[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124%E2%80%9325-279) Most of the new directions in AI relied heavily on mathematical models, including [artificial neural networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_networks "Artificial neural networks"), [probabilistic reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probabilistic_reasoning "Probabilistic reasoning"), [soft computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_computing "Soft computing") and [reinforcement learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reinforcement_learning "Reinforcement learning"). In the 90s and 2000s, many other highly mathematical tools were adapted for AI. These tools were applied to machine learning, perception, and mobility.

There was a widespread realization that many of the problems that AI needed to solve were already being worked on by researchers in fields like [statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics "Statistics"), [mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics "Mathematics"), [electrical engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_engineering "Electrical engineering"), [economics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economics "Economics"), or [operations research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operations_research "Operations research"). The shared mathematical language allowed both a higher level of collaboration with more established and successful fields and the achievement of results that were measurable and provable; AI had become a more rigorous "scientific" discipline. Another key reason for the success in the 90s was that AI researchers focused on specific problems with verifiable solutions (an approach later derided as _[narrow AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narrow\_AI "Narrow AI")_). This provided useful tools in the present, rather than speculation about the future.

A new paradigm called "[intelligent agents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligent_agent "Intelligent agent")" became widely accepted during the 1990s.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004471%E2%80%93478-280)[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021Chapter_2-281)[[ah]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-283) Although earlier researchers had proposed modular "divide and conquer" approaches to AI,[[ai]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-284) the intelligent agent did not reach its modern form until [Judea Pearl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judea_Pearl "Judea Pearl"), [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell"), [Leslie P. Kaelbling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leslie_P._Kaelbling "Leslie P. Kaelbling"), and others brought concepts from [decision theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decision_theory "Decision theory") and economics into the study of AI.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202161-282) When the [economist's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economics "Economics") definition of a [rational agent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_agent "Rational agent") was married to [computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science")'s definition of an [object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming") or [module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Module_(programming) "Module (programming)"), the intelligent agent paradigm was complete.

An [intelligent agent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligent_agent "Intelligent agent") is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions which maximize its chances of success. By this definition, simple programs that solve specific problems are "intelligent agents", as are human beings and organizations of human beings, such as [firms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firm "Firm"). The intelligent agent paradigm defines AI research as "the study of intelligent agents".[[aj]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-285) This is a generalization of some earlier definitions of AI: it goes beyond studying human intelligence; it studies all kinds of intelligence. The paradigm gave researchers license to study isolated problems and to disagree about methods, but still retain hope that their work could be combined into an [agent architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agent_architecture "Agent architecture") that would be capable of general intelligence.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004478-286)

### Milestones and Moore's law

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Milestones and Moore's law")]

On 11 May 1997, [Deep Blue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue "IBM Deep Blue") became the first computer chess-playing system to beat a reigning world chess champion, [Garry Kasparov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garry_Kasparov "Garry Kasparov").[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004480%E2%80%93483-287) In 2005, a Stanford robot won the [DARPA Grand Challenge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA_Grand_Challenge "DARPA Grand Challenge") by driving autonomously for 131 miles along an unrehearsed desert trail. Two years later, a team from CMU won the [DARPA Urban Challenge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA_Urban_Challenge "DARPA Urban Challenge") by autonomously navigating 55 miles in an urban environment while responding to traffic hazards and adhering to traffic laws.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202128-288)

These successes were not due to some revolutionary new paradigm, but mostly to the tedious application of engineering skill and to the tremendous increase in the speed and capacity of computers by the 90s.[[ak]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-290) In fact, [Deep Blue's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue "IBM Deep Blue") computer was 10 million times faster than the [Ferranti Mark 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferranti_Mark_1 "Ferranti Mark 1") that [Christopher Strachey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Strachey "Christopher Strachey") taught to play chess in 1951.[[al]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-291) This dramatic increase is measured by [Moore's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_law "Moore's law"), which predicts that the speed and memory capacity of computers double every two years. The fundamental problem of "raw computer power" was slowly being overcome.

### Arts and literature influenced by AI

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Arts and literature influenced by AI")]

[Electronic literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_literature "Electronic literature") experiments such as _[The Impermanence Agent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Impermanence\_Agent "The Impermanence Agent")_ (1998–2002) and digital art such as _[Agent Ruby](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agent\_Ruby "Agent Ruby")_ used AI in their art and literature, "laying bare the bias accompanying forms of technology that feign objectivity."[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-292)

Big data, deep learning, AGI (2005–2017)
----------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Big data, deep learning, AGI (2005–2017)")]

In the first decades of the 21st century, access to large amounts of data (known as "[big data](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_data "Big data")"), [cheaper and faster computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_law "Moore's law") and advanced [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning") techniques were successfully applied to many problems throughout the economy. A turning point was the success of [deep learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_learning "Deep learning") around 2012, which improved the performance of machine learning on many tasks, including image and video processing, text analysis, and speech recognition.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeCunBengioHinton2015-293) Investment in AI increased along with its capabilities, and by 2016, the market for AI-related products, hardware, and software reached more than $8 billion, and the _New York Times_ reported that interest in AI had reached a "frenzy".[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELohr2016-294)

In 2002, [Ben Goertzel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben_Goertzel "Ben Goertzel") and others became concerned that AI had largely abandoned its original goal of producing versatile, fully intelligent machines, and argued in favor of more direct research into [artificial general intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence "Artificial general intelligence") (AGI). By the mid-2010s, several companies and institutions had been founded to pursue artificial general intelligence, such as [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI") and [Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google "Google")'s [DeepMind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DeepMind "DeepMind"). During the same period, new insights into [superintelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superintelligence "Superintelligence") raised concerns that AI was an [existential threat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_from_artificial_general_intelligence "Existential risk from artificial general intelligence"). The risks and unintended consequences of AI technology became an area of serious academic research after 2016.

### Big data and big machines

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Big data and big machines")]

The success of machine learning in the 2000s depended on the availability of vast amounts of training data and faster computers.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126%E2%80%9327-295) Russell and Norvig wrote that the "improvement in performance obtained by increasing the size of the data set by two or three orders of magnitude outweighs any improvement that can be made by tweaking the algorithm."[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126-228)[Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton") recalled that back in the 80s and 90s, the problem was that "our labeled datasets were thousands of times too small. [And] our computers were millions of times too slow."[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-296) This was no longer true by 2010.

The most useful data in the 2000s came from curated, labeled data sets created specifically for machine learning and AI. In 2007, a group at [UMass Amherst](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Massachusetts_Amherst "University of Massachusetts Amherst") released "Labeled Faces in the Wild", an annotated set of images of faces that was widely used to train and test [face recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_recognition "Face recognition") systems for the next several decades.[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202031-297)[Fei-Fei Li](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fei-Fei_Li "Fei-Fei Li") developed [ImageNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ImageNet "ImageNet"), a database of three million images captioned by volunteers using the [Amazon Mechanical Turk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_Mechanical_Turk "Amazon Mechanical Turk"). Released in 2009, it was a useful body of training data and a benchmark for testing for the next generation of image processing systems.[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202022%E2%80%9323-298)[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126-228) Google released [word2vec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word2vec "Word2vec") in 2013 as an open source resource. It used large amounts of data text scraped from the internet and [word embedding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word_embedding "Word embedding") to create a numeric vector to represent each word. Users were surprised at how well it was able to capture word meanings, for example, ordinary vector addition would give equivalences like China + River = Yangtze or London − England + France = Paris.[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian20206-299) This database in particular would be essential for the development of [large language models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_language_model "Large language model") in the late 2010s.

The explosive growth of the internet gave machine learning programs access to billions of pages of text and images that could be [scraped](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data_scraping "Data scraping"). And, for specific problems, large privately held databases contained the relevant data. [McKinsey Global Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McKinsey_Global_Institute "McKinsey Global Institute") reported that "by 2009, nearly all sectors in the US economy had at least an average of 200 terabytes of stored data".[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKinsey_&_Co2011-300) This collection of information was known in the 2000s as _[big data](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big\_data "Big data")_.

In a _[Jeopardy!](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeopardy! "Jeopardy!")_ exhibition match in February 2011, [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM")'s [question answering system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Question_answering_system "Question answering system")[Watson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_Watson "IBM Watson") defeated the two best _Jeopardy!_ champions, [Brad Rutter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brad_Rutter "Brad Rutter") and [Ken Jennings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ken_Jennings "Ken Jennings"), by a significant margin.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkoff2011-301) Watson's expertise would have been impossible without the information available on the internet.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126-228)

In 2012, [AlexNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlexNet "AlexNet"), a [deep learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_learning "Deep learning") model,[[am]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-303) developed by [Alex Krizhevsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alex_Krizhevsky "Alex Krizhevsky"), won the [ImageNet Large Scale Visual Recognition Challenge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ImageNet_Large_Scale_Visual_Recognition_Challenge "ImageNet Large Scale Visual Recognition Challenge"), with significantly fewer errors than the second-place winner.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202024-304)[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126-228) Krizhevsky worked with [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton") at the [University of Toronto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Toronto "University of Toronto").[[an]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-305) This was a turning point in machine learning: over the next few years, dozens of other approaches to image recognition were abandoned in favor of deep learning.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126%E2%80%9327-295)

Deep learning uses a multi-layer [perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perceptron "Perceptron"). Although this architecture has been known since the 60s, getting it to work requires powerful hardware and large amounts of training data.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202127-306) Before these became available, improving the performance of image processing systems required hand-crafted _ad hoc_ features that were difficult to implement.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202127-306) Deep learning was simpler and more general.[[ao]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-307)

Deep learning was applied to dozens of problems over the next few years (such as speech recognition, machine translation, medical diagnosis, and game playing). In every case, it showed enormous gains in performance.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126%E2%80%9327-295) Investment and interest in AI boomed as a result.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202126%E2%80%9327-295)

### The alignment problem

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: The alignment problem")]

It became fashionable in the 2000s to begin talking about the future of AI again and several popular books considered the possibility of [superintelligent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superintelligent "Superintelligent") machines and what they might mean for human society. Some of this was optimistic (such as [Ray Kurzweil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil "Ray Kurzweil")'s _[The Singularity is Near](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Singularity\_is\_Near "The Singularity is Near")_), but others warned that a sufficiently powerful AI was [existential threat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_of_artificial_general_intelligence "Existential risk of artificial general intelligence") to humanity, such as [Nick Bostrom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom "Nick Bostrom") and [Eliezer Yudkowsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eliezer_Yudkowsky "Eliezer Yudkowsky").[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202133,_1004-308) The topic became widely covered in the press and many leading intellectuals and politicians commented on the issue.

AI programs in the 21st century are defined by their [goals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utility_function "Utility function")—the specific measures that they are designed to optimize. [Nick Bostrom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom "Nick Bostrom")'s influential 2014 book _[Superintelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superintelligence\_(book) "Superintelligence (book)")_[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-309) argued that, if one isn't careful about defining these goals, the machine may cause harm to humanity in the process of achieving a goal. [Stuart J. Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stuart_J._Russell "Stuart J. Russell") used the example of an intelligent robot that kills its owner to prevent it from being unplugged, reasoning "you can't fetch the coffee if you're dead".[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell2020-310) (This problem is known by the technical term "[instrumental convergence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instrumental_convergence "Instrumental convergence")".) The solution is to _align_ the machine's goal function with the goals of its owner and humanity in general. Thus, the problem of mitigating the risks and unintended consequences of AI became known as "the value alignment problem" or AI alignment.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20215,_33,_1002%E2%80%931003-311)

At the same time, machine learning systems had begun to have disturbing unintended consequences. [Cathy O'Neil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathy_O%27Neil "Cathy O'Neil") explained how statistical algorithms had been among the causes of the [2008 economic crash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_recession "Great recession"),[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Neill2016-312)[Julia Angwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julia_Angwin "Julia Angwin") of [ProPublica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ProPublica "ProPublica") argued that the [COMPAS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/COMPAS_(software) "COMPAS (software)") system used by the criminal justice system exhibited racial bias under some measures,[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202060%E2%80%9361-313)[[ap]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-315) others showed that many machine learning systems exhibited some form of racial [bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithmic_bias "Algorithmic bias"),[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian20206%E2%80%937,_25-316) and there were many other examples of dangerous outcomes that had resulted from machine learning systems.[[aq]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-317)

In 2016, the election of [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") and the controversy over the COMPAS system illuminated several problems with the current technological infrastructure, including misinformation, social media algorithms designed to maximize engagement, the misuse of personal data and the trustworthiness of predictive models.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202067-318) Issues of [fairness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fairness_(machine_learning) "Fairness (machine learning)") and unintended consequences became significantly more popular at AI conferences, publications vastly increased, funding became available, and many researchers refocused their careers on these issues. The [value alignment problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_alignment "AI alignment") became a serious field of academic study.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202067,_73,_117-319)[[ar]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-321)

### Artificial general intelligence research

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Artificial general intelligence research")]

In the early 2000s, several researchers became concerned that mainstream AI was too focused on "measurable performance in specific applications"[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202132-322) (known as "[narrow AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narrow_AI "Narrow AI")") and had abandoned AI's original goal of creating versatile, fully intelligent machines. An early critic was [Nils Nilsson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson "Nils John Nilsson") in 1995, and similar opinions were published by AI elder statesmen John McCarthy, Marvin Minsky, and Patrick Winston in 2007–2009. Minsky organized a symposium on "human-level AI" in 2004.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202132-322)[Ben Goertzel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ben_Goertzel "Ben Goertzel") adopted the term "[artificial general intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence "Artificial general intelligence")" for the new sub-field, founding a journal and holding conferences beginning in 2008.[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202133-323) The new field grew rapidly, buoyed by the continuing success of artificial neural networks and the hope that it was the key to AGI.

Several competing companies, laboratories and foundations were founded to develop AGI in the 2010s. [DeepMind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DeepMind "DeepMind") was founded in 2010 by three English scientists, [Demis Hassabis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demis_Hassabis "Demis Hassabis"), [Shane Legg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shane_Legg "Shane Legg") and [Mustafa Suleyman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustafa_Suleyman "Mustafa Suleyman"), with funding from [Peter Thiel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Thiel "Peter Thiel") and later [Elon Musk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elon_Musk "Elon Musk"). The founders and financiers were deeply concerned about [AI safety](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_safety "AI safety") and the [existential risk of AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_of_AI "Existential risk of AI"). DeepMind's founders had a personal connection with Yudkowsky, and Musk was among those who were actively raising the alarm.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324) Hassabis was both worried about the dangers of AGI and optimistic about its power; he hoped they could "solve AI, then solve everything else."[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202131-325)_The New York Times_ wrote in 2023, "At the heart of this competition is a brain-stretching paradox. The people who say they are most worried about AI are among the most determined to create it and enjoy its riches. They have justified their ambition with their strong belief that they alone can keep AI from endangering Earth."[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324)

In 2012, [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton") (who had been leading neural network research since the 80s) was approached by [Baidu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baidu "Baidu"), which wanted to hire him and all his students for an enormous sum. Hinton decided to hold an auction and, at a Lake Tahoe AI conference, they sold themselves to [Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google "Google") for a price of $44 million. Hassabis took notice and sold DeepMind to Google in 2014, on the condition that it would not accept military contracts and would be overseen by an ethics board.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324)

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Disrupt_SF_TechCrunch_Disrupt_San_Francisco_2019_-_Day_2_(48838377432).jpg)

[Sam Altman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sam_Altman "Sam Altman"), the co-founder and CEO of [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI")

[Larry Page](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_Page "Larry Page") of Google, unlike Musk and Hassabis, was an optimist about the future of AI. Musk and Page became embroiled in an argument about the risk of AGI at Musk's 2015 birthday party. They had been friends for decades, but stopped speaking to each other shortly afterwards. Musk attended the only meeting of DeepMind's ethics board, where it became clear that Google was uninterested in mitigating the harm of AGI. Frustrated by his lack of influence, he founded [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI") in 2015, enlisting [Sam Altman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sam_Altman "Sam Altman") to run it and hiring top scientists. OpenAI began as a non-profit, "free from the economic incentives that were driving Google and other corporations."[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324) Musk became frustrated again and left the company in 2018. OpenAI turned to Microsoft for continued financial support and Altman and OpenAI formed a for-profit version of the company with more than $1 billion in financing.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324)

In 2021, [Dario Amodei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dario_Amodei "Dario Amodei") and 14 other scientists left OpenAI over concerns that the company was putting profits above safety. They formed [Anthropic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropic "Anthropic"), which soon had $6 billion in financing from Microsoft and Google.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324)

Large language models, AI boom (2017–present)
---------------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: Large language models, AI boom (2017–present)")]

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2024_AI_patents_by_country_-_artificial_intelligence.svg)

In 2024, AI patents in China and the US numbered more than three-fourths of AI patents worldwide.[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-RandDworld_20241103-326) Though China had more AI patents, the US had 35% more patents per AI patent-applicant company than China.[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-RandDworld_20241103-326)

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:20250202_%22AI%22_(search_term)_on_Google_Trends.svg)

The number of Google searches for the term "AI" accelerated in 2022.

The AI boom started with the initial development of key architectures and algorithms such as the [transformer architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transformer_architecture "Transformer architecture") in 2017, leading to the scaling and development of large language models exhibiting human-like traits of knowledge, attention, and creativity. The new AI era began in 2020, with the public release of a scaled [large language model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_language_model "Large language model") (LLM) GPT-3, the predecessor of [ChatGPT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ChatGPT "ChatGPT").[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-327)

### Transformer architecture and large language models

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Transformer architecture and large language models")]

In 2017, the [transformer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transformer_(machine_learning_model) "Transformer (machine learning model)") architecture was proposed by Google researchers in a paper titled "[Attention Is All You Need](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attention_Is_All_You_Need "Attention Is All You Need")". It exploits a [self-attention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attention_(machine_learning)#Self-attention "Attention (machine learning)") mechanism and became widely used in large language models.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurgia2023-328) Large language models, based on the transformer, were further developed by other companies: [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI") released [GPT-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPT-3 "GPT-3") in 2020, then [DeepMind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DeepMind "DeepMind") released [Gato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gato_(DeepMind) "Gato (DeepMind)") in 2022. These are [foundation models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundation_models "Foundation models"): they are trained on vast quantities of unlabeled data and can be adapted to a wide range of downstream tasks. These models can discuss a huge number of topics and display general knowledge, which has raised questions around whether or not they are examples of [artificial general intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence "Artificial general intelligence").

[Bill Gates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Gates "Bill Gates") was skeptical of the new technology and the hype that surrounded AGI. However, Altman presented him with a live demo of [ChatGPT-4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPT-4 "GPT-4") passing an advanced biology test. Gates was convinced.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetzWeiseGrantIsaac2023-324) In 2023, [Microsoft Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft_Research "Microsoft Research") tested the model with a large variety of tasks, and concluded that "it could reasonably be viewed as an early (yet still incomplete) version of an [artificial general intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence "Artificial general intelligence") (AGI) system".[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBubeckChandrasekaranEldanGehrke2023-329)
