In 2024, [OpenAI o3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI_o3 "OpenAI o3"), a type of advanced [reasoning model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning_language_model "Reasoning language model") developed by OpenAI, was announced. On the Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus for Artificial General Intelligence (ARC-AGI) benchmark developed by [François Chollet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Chollet "François Chollet") in 2019, the model achieved an unofficial score of 87.5% on the semi-private test, surpassing the typical human score of 84%. The benchmark is supposed to be a necessary, but not sufficient, test for AGI. Speaking of the benchmark, Chollet has said, "You'll know AGI is here when the exercise of creating tasks that are easy for regular humans but hard for AI becomes simply impossible."[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-330)

Investment in AI grew exponentially after 2020, with venture capital funding for generative AI companies increasing dramatically. Total AI investments rose from $18 billion in 2014 to $119 billion in 2021, with generative AI accounting for approximately 30% of investments by 2023.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-331) According to metrics from 2017 to 2021, the United States outranked the rest of the world in terms of [venture capital funding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venture_capital_funding "Venture capital funding"), number of [startups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Startups "Startups"), and AI [patents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patents "Patents") granted.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-:02-332) The commercial AI scene became dominated by American [Big Tech](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Tech "Big Tech") companies, whose investments in this area surpassed those from U.S.-based [venture capitalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venture_capitalists "Venture capitalists").[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-333)[OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI")'s valuation reached $86 billion by early 2024,[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-334) while [NVIDIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NVIDIA "NVIDIA")'s market capitalization surpassed $3.3 trillion by mid-2024, making it the world's largest company by [market capitalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Market_capitalization "Market capitalization") as the demand for AI-capable [GPUs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPU "GPU") surged.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-335)

### Advent of AI for public use

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Advent of AI for public use")]

[15.ai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/15.ai "15.ai"), launched in March 2020[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-336) by an anonymous [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT") researcher,[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-337)[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-338) was one of the earliest examples of [generative AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generative_AI "Generative AI") gaining widespread public attention during the initial stages of the AI boom.[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-339) The free [web application](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application "Web application") demonstrated the ability to clone character voices using neural networks with minimal training data, requiring as little as 15 seconds of audio to reproduce a voice—a capability later corroborated by [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI") in 2024.[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-340) The service went [viral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_phenomenon "Viral phenomenon") on social media platforms in early 2021,[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-341)[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-342) allowing users to generate speech for characters from [popular media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_media "Popular media") franchises, and became particularly notable for its pioneering role in popularizing [AI voice synthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_learning_speech_synthesis "Deep learning speech synthesis") for [creative content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Content_creation "Content creation") and [memes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_meme "Internet meme").[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-343)

> Contemporary AI systems are now becoming human-competitive at general tasks, and we must ask ourselves: Should we let machines flood our information channels with propaganda and untruth? Should we automate away all the jobs, including the fulfilling ones? Should we develop nonhuman minds that might eventually outnumber, outsmart, obsolete and replace us? Should we risk loss of control of our civilization? Such decisions must not be delegated to unelected tech leaders. **Powerful AI systems should be developed only once we are confident that their effects will be positive and their risks will be manageable.** This confidence must be well justified and increase with the magnitude of a system's potential effects. OpenAI's recent statement regarding artificial general intelligence states that "At some point, it may be important to get an independent review before starting to train future systems, and for the most advanced efforts to agree to limit the rate of growth of compute used for creating new models." We agree. That point is now.
> 
> 
> Therefore, **we call on all AI labs to immediately pause for at least 6 months the training of AI systems more powerful than GPT-4**. This pause should be public and verifiable, and include all key actors. If such a pause cannot be enacted quickly, governments should step in and institute a moratorium.

[ChatGPT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ChatGPT "ChatGPT") was launched on 30 November 2022, marking a pivotal moment in artificial intelligence's public adoption. Within days of its release it went viral, gaining over 100 million users in two months and becoming the fastest-growing consumer software application in history.[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-:8-345) The chatbot's ability to engage in human-like conversations, write code, and generate creative content captured public imagination and led to rapid adoption across various sectors including [education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_in_education "AI in education"), [business](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_industry "Artificial intelligence in industry"), and research.[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-346) ChatGPT's success prompted unprecedented responses from major technology companies—[Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google "Google") declared a "code red" and rapidly launched [Gemini](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Gemini "Google Gemini") (formerly known as Google Bard), while [Microsoft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft "Microsoft") incorporated the technology into [Bing Chat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bing_Chat "Bing Chat").[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-347)

The rapid adoption of these AI technologies sparked intense debate about their implications. Notable AI researchers and industry leaders voiced both optimism and concern about the accelerating pace of development. In March 2023, over 20,000 signatories, including [computer scientist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_scientist "Computer scientist")[Yoshua Bengio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yoshua_Bengio "Yoshua Bengio"), [Elon Musk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elon_Musk "Elon Musk"), and [Apple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apple_Inc. "Apple Inc.") co-founder [Steve Wozniak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Wozniak "Steve Wozniak"), signed [an open letter calling for a pause in advanced AI development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pause_Giant_AI_Experiments:_An_Open_Letter "Pause Giant AI Experiments: An Open Letter"), citing "[profound risks to society and humanity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_from_artificial_intelligence "Existential risk from artificial intelligence")."[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-348) However, other prominent researchers like [Juergen Schmidhuber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juergen_Schmidhuber "Juergen Schmidhuber") took a more optimistic view, emphasizing that the majority of AI research aims to make "human lives longer and healthier and easier."[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-guardian2023-349)

By mid-2024, however, the financial sector began to scrutinize AI companies more closely, particularly questioning their capacity to produce a [return on investment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Return_on_investment "Return on investment") commensurate with their massive valuations. Some prominent investors raised concerns about market expectations becoming disconnected from fundamental business realities. [Jeremy Grantham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremy_Grantham "Jeremy Grantham"), co-founder of [GMO LLC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GMO_LLC "GMO LLC"), warned investors to "be quite careful" and drew parallels to previous technology-driven market bubbles.[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-350) Similarly, [Jeffrey Gundlach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeffrey_Gundlach "Jeffrey Gundlach"), CEO of [DoubleLine Capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DoubleLine_Capital "DoubleLine Capital"), explicitly compared the AI boom to the [dot-com bubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot-com_bubble "Dot-com bubble") of the late 1990s, suggesting that investor enthusiasm might be outpacing realistic near-term capabilities and revenue potential.[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-351) These concerns were amplified by the substantial market capitalizations of AI-focused companies, many of which had yet to demonstrate sustainable profitability models.

In March 2024, [Anthropic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropic "Anthropic") released the [Claude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_(AI) "Claude (AI)") 3 family of large language models, including Claude 3 Haiku, Sonnet, and Opus.[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-352) The models demonstrated significant improvements in capabilities across various benchmarks, with Claude 3 Opus notably outperforming leading models from OpenAI and Google.[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-353) In June 2024, Anthropic released Claude 3.5 Sonnet, which demonstrated improved performance compared to the larger Claude 3 Opus, particularly in areas such as coding, multistep workflows, and image analysis.[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-354)

In 2024, the [Royal Swedish Academy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy "Royal Swedish Academy") of Sciences awarded [Nobel Prizes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prizes "Nobel Prizes") in recognition of groundbreaking contributions to [artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence "Artificial intelligence"). The recipients included:

*   In physics: [John Hopfield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Hopfield "John Hopfield") for his work on physics-inspired [Hopfield networks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hopfield_network "Hopfield network"), and [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton") for foundational contributions to [Boltzmann machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boltzmann_machine "Boltzmann machine") and [deep learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_learning "Deep learning").
*   In chemistry: [David Baker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Baker_(biochemist) "David Baker (biochemist)"), [Demis Hassabis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demis_Hassabis "Demis Hassabis"), and [John Jumper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_M._Jumper "John M. Jumper") for their advancements in [protein folding predictions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_structure_prediction "Protein structure prediction"). See [AlphaFold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlphaFold "AlphaFold").[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-355)

### Further study and development of AI

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_artificial_intelligence&action=edit&section=55 "Edit section: Further study and development of AI")]

In January 2025, OpenAI announced a new AI, ChatGPT-Gov, which would be specifically designed for US government agencies to use securely.[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-chatgpt-gov-356) Open AI said that agencies could utilize ChatGPT Gov on a Microsoft Azure cloud or Azure Government cloud, "on top of Microsoft's Azure OpenAI Service." OpenAI's announcement stated that "Self-hosting ChatGPT Gov enables agencies to more easily manage their own security, privacy, and compliance requirements, such as stringent cybersecurity frameworks (IL5, CJIS, ITAR, [FedRAMP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FedRAMP "FedRAMP") High). Additionally, we believe this infrastructure will expedite internal authorization of OpenAI's tools for the handling of non-public sensitive data."[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-chatgpt-gov-356)

Countries have invested in policies and funding to deploy [autonomous robots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autonomous_robot "Autonomous robot") in an attempt to address labor shortages and enhance efficiency, while also implementing [regulatory frameworks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regulation_of_artificial_intelligence "Regulation of artificial intelligence") for ethical and safe development.

In 2025, China invested approximately 730 billion yuan (roughly US$100 billion) to advance AI and robotics in smart manufacturing and healthcare.[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-357) The "14th Five-Year Plan" (2021–2025) prioritized service robots, with AI systems enabling robots to perform complex tasks like assisting in surgeries or automating factory assembly lines.[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-358) Some funding also supported defense applications, such as autonomous drones.[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-359)[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-360) Starting in September 2025, China mandated labeling of AI-generated content to ensure transparency and public trust in these technologies.[[317]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-361)

In January 2025, [Stargate LLC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stargate_LLC "Stargate LLC") was formed as a joint venture of [OpenAI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI "OpenAI"), [SoftBank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SoftBank_Group "SoftBank Group"), [Oracle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oracle_Corporation "Oracle Corporation"), and [MGX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MGX_Fund_Management_Limited "MGX Fund Management Limited"), who announced plans to invest US$500 billion in AI infrastructure in the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") by 2029. The venture was formally announced by U.S. President Donald Trump on 21 January 2025, with SoftBank CEO [Masayoshi Son](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masayoshi_Son "Masayoshi Son") appointed as chairman.[[318]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-reuters-362)[[319]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-363)

The U.S. government allocated approximately $2 billion to integrate AI and robotics in manufacturing and logistics.[[320]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-364)[_[non-primary source needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:No\_original\_research#Primary,\_secondary\_and\_tertiary\_sources "Wikipedia:No original research")_] State governments supplemented this with funding for service robots, such as those deployed in warehouses to fulfill verbal commands for inventory management or in eldercare facilities to respond to residents' requests for assistance.[[321]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-365) Some funds were directed to defense, including [lethal autonomous weapon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lethal_autonomous_weapon "Lethal autonomous weapon") and [military robots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_robot "Military robot"). In January 2025, Executive Order 14179 established an "AI Action Plan" to accelerate innovation and deployment of these technologies with the declared intent of "world domination" and "victory".[[322]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-366)[[323]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-TrumpAI-367)

*   [History of knowledge representation and reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_knowledge_representation_and_reasoning "History of knowledge representation and reasoning")

*   [Timeline of machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_machine_learning "Timeline of machine learning")

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-38 "Jump up")**The [Lambda calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lambda_calculus "Lambda calculus") was an inspiration for the [Lisp programming language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lisp_programming_language "Lisp programming language"), which was important to the development of 20th-century AI).[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993190_196,_61-37)
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-59 "Jump up")**Alan Turing was thinking about machine intelligence at least as early as 1941, when he circulated a paper on machine intelligence which could be the earliest paper in the field of AI — although it is now lost. His 1950 paper was followed by three radio broadcasts on AI by Turing, the two lectures 'Intelligent Machinery, A Heretical Theory' and 'Can Digital Computers Think?' and the panel discussion 'Can Automatic Calculating Machines be Said to Think?'[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland2004-55)
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-73 "Jump up")**This was an early statement of the philosophical position [John Searle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Searle "John Searle") would later call the "[Strong AI hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strong_AI_hypothesis "Strong AI hypothesis")": that machines can contain minds just as human bodies do.
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-77 "Jump up")**[Daniel Crevier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Crevier "Daniel Crevier") wrote "[the proposal] later became known as the 'physical symbol systems hypothesis'".[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199348-75) The [physical symbol system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_symbol_system "Physical symbol system") hypothesis was articulated and named by [Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon") in their paper on [GPS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Problem_Solver "General Problem Solver").[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewellSimon1995-76) It includes a more specific definition of a "machine" as an agent that manipulates symbols.
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-81 "Jump up")** "I won't swear and I hadn't seen it before," McCarthy told [Pamela McCorduck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pamela_McCorduck "Pamela McCorduck") in 1979.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004114-78) However, [McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") also stated unequivocally "I came up with the term" in a [CNET](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CNET "CNET") interview.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkillings2006-79) The term was chosen by McCarthy to avoid associations with [cybernetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cybernetics "Cybernetics") and the influence of [Norbert Wiener](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norbert_Wiener "Norbert Wiener"). "[O]ne of the reasons for inventing the term "artificial intelligence" was to escape association with "cybernetics". Its concentration on analog feedback seemed misguided, and I wished to avoid having either to accept Norbert (not Robert) Wiener as a guru or having to argue with him.".[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199673-80)
6.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-83 "Jump up")**Pamela McCorduck discusses how the Dartmouth conference alumni dominated the first two decades of AI research, calling them the "invisible college".[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004129%E2%80%93130-82)
7.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-86 "Jump up")**[Daniel Crevier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Crevier "Daniel Crevier") wrote "the conference is generally recognized as the official birthdate of the new science."[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199349-85)
8.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-89 "Jump up")**There were a few psychologists who avoided behaviorism and embraced a cognitive approach before it was fashionable, such as [Frederic Bartlett](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederic_Bartlett "Frederic Bartlett") and [Kenneth Craig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_Craig "Kenneth Craig")[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202113%E2%80%9314-88)
9.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-92 "Jump up")**Russell and Norvig wrote that "it was astonishing whenever a computer did anything remotely clever".[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig200318-90) AI-founder [John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") called this the "Look, Ma, no hands!" era.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202118-91)
10.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-111 "Jump up")**This avoided the commonsense knowledge problem, discussed below.
11.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-124 "Jump up")**The hardware diversity was particularly clear in the different technologies used in implementing the adjustable weights. The perceptron machines and the [SNARC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_Neural_Analog_Reinforcement_Calculator "Stochastic Neural Analog Reinforcement Calculator") used [potentiometers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potentiometer "Potentiometer") moved by electric motors. ADALINE used [memistors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Memistor "Memistor") adjusted by [electroplating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroplating "Electroplating"), though they also used simulations on an [IBM 1620](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_1620 "IBM 1620") computer. The MINOS machines used [ferrite cores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic-core_memory "Magnetic-core memory") with multiple holes in them that could be individually blocked, with the degree of blockage representing the weights.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlazaran_Rodriguez1991-123)
12.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-136 "Jump up")**Minsky strongly believes he was misquoted.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004272%E2%80%93274-134)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199396-135)
13.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-142 "Jump up")**McCorduck also notes that funding was mostly under the direction of alumni of the [Dartmouth workshop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmouth_workshop "Dartmouth workshop") of 1956.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004131-141)
14.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-152 "Jump up")**Russell and Norvig wrote "in almost all cases, these early systems failed on more difficult tasks."[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202121-151)
15.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-155 "Jump up")**[Bruce Buchanan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bruce_Buchanan "Bruce Buchanan") wrote: "Early programs were necessarily limited in scope by the size and speed of memory"[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBuchanan200556-154)
16.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-159 "Jump up")**History would prove Moravec right about applications like computer vision. Moravec estimated that simply matching the [edge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edge_detection "Edge detection") and [motion detection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motion_detection "Motion detection") capabilities of the [human retina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_retina "Human retina") in [real time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real-time_computing "Real-time computing") would require a general-purpose computer capable of 1000 [million instructions per second](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Million_instructions_per_second "Million instructions per second") (MIPS). In 1976, the fastest supercomputer, the $8 million [Cray-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cray-1 "Cray-1") was only capable of 130 MIPS, and a typical desktop computer had 1 MIPS. As of 2011, practical computer vision applications require 10,000 to 1,000,000 MIPS.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec2000-158)
17.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-164 "Jump up")**Such as the [frame](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frame_problem "Frame problem"), [ramification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramification_problem "Ramification problem") and [qualification problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qualification_problem "Qualification problem"), as well as the difficulty of [default reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Default_reasoning "Default reasoning") and [word-sense disambiguation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word-sense_disambiguation "Word-sense disambiguation").
18.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-165 "Jump up")**Russell and Norvig write: "[M]any of the concepts we name in language fail, on closer inspection, to have the logically defined necessary and sufficient conditions that early AI researchers hoped to capture in axiomatic form."[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-128)
19.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-170 "Jump up")**[John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") wrote in response that "the combinatorial explosion problem has been recognized in AI from the beginning"[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy1974-169)
20.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-172 "Jump up")**This account is based on [Crevier 1993](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFCrevier1993), pp.115–116. Other views include [McCorduck 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFMcCorduck2004), pp.306–313 and [NRC 1999](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFNRC1999) under "Success in Speech Recognition".
21.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-174 "Jump up")**Moravec explains, "Their initial promises to DARPA had been much too optimistic. Of course, what they delivered stopped considerably short of that. But they felt they couldn't in their next proposal promise less than in the first one, so they promised more."[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993115-173)
22.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-177 "Jump up")**While the autonomous tank was a failure, the battle management system (called "[DART](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_Analysis_and_Replanning_Tool "Dynamic Analysis and Replanning Tool")") proved to be enormously successful, saving billions in the first [Gulf War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_War "Gulf War"), repaying the investment and justifying the [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA")'s pragmatic policy, at least as far as [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") was concerned.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENRC1999-176)
23.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-180 "Jump up")**"Know-how" is Dreyfus' term. Dreyfus makes a distinction between "knowing how" and "knowing that", a modern version of [Heidegger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heidegger "Heidegger")'s distinction of [ready-to-hand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ready-to-hand "Ready-to-hand") and [present-at-hand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Present-at-hand "Present-at-hand").[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyfusDreyfus1986-179)
24.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-186 "Jump up")**Weizenbaum said: "I became the only member of the AI community to be seen eating lunch with Dreyfus. And I deliberately made it plain that theirs was not the way to treat a human being."[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993123-185)
25.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-191 "Jump up")**Colby and his colleagues later also developed [chatterbot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chatterbot "Chatterbot")-like "computer simulations of paranoid processes ([PARRY](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARRY "PARRY"))" to "make intelligible paranoid processes in explicit symbol processing terms."[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColby19746-190)
26.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-197 "Jump up")**[Wason & Shapiro (1966)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFWasonShapiro1966) showed that people do poorly on completely abstract problems, but if the problem is restated to allow the use of intuitive [social intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_intelligence "Social intelligence"), performance dramatically improves. (See [Wason selection task](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wason_selection_task "Wason selection task")) [Kahneman, Slovic & Tversky (1982)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFKahnemanSlovicTversky1982) have shown that people are terrible at elementary problems that involve uncertain reasoning. (See [list of cognitive biases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_cognitive_biases "List of cognitive biases") for several examples). [Eleanor Rosch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleanor_Rosch "Eleanor Rosch")'s work is described in [Lakoff 1987](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFLakoff1987). Kahnmann published a more general theory of symbolic cognition and other kinds of thinking in his book _[Thinking Fast and Slow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thinking\_Fast\_and\_Slow "Thinking Fast and Slow")_ (2011)
27.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-198 "Jump up")** An early example of [McCarthy's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") position was in the journal [Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_(journal) "Science (journal)") where he said "This is AI, so we don't care if it's psychologically real" ([Kolata 1982](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFKolata1982)), and he recently reiterated his position at the [AI@50](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI@50 "AI@50") conference where he said "Artificial intelligence is not, by definition, simulation of human intelligence" ([Maker 2006](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFMaker2006)).
28.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-200 "Jump up")**Another aspect of the conflict was called "the procedural/declarative distinction" but did not prove to be influential in later AI research.
29.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-223 "Jump up")**Versions of backpropagation had been developed in several fields, most directly as the reverse mode of [automatic differentiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automatic_differentiation "Automatic differentiation") published by [Seppo Linnainmaa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seppo_Linnainmaa "Seppo Linnainmaa") (1970). It was applied to neural networks in the 1970s by [Paul Werbos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Werbos "Paul Werbos").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmidhuber2022-127)
30.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-232 "Jump up")**[Hans Moravec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Moravec "Hans Moravec") wrote: "I am confident that this bottom-up route to artificial intelligence will one date meet the traditional top-down route more than half way, ready to provide the real world competence and the commonsense knowledge that has been so frustratingly elusive in reasoning programs. Fully intelligent machines will result when the metaphorical [golden spike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_spike "Golden spike") is driven, uniting the two efforts."[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec198820-231)
31.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-259 "Jump up")**[AI winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_winter "AI winter") was first used as the title of a seminar on the subject for the [Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Artificial_Intelligence "Association for the Advancement of Artificial Intelligence").[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993203-258)
32.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-265 "Jump up")**McCorduck writes "Two and a half decades later, we can see that the Japanese didn't quite meet all of those ambitious goals."[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004441-264)
33.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-267 "Jump up")**See [Applications of artificial intelligence §Computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applications_of_artificial_intelligence#Computer_science "Applications of artificial intelligence")
34.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-283 "Jump up")**Russell and Norvig wrote "The whole-agent view is now widely accepted."[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202161-282)
35.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-284 "Jump up")**[Carl Hewitt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Hewitt "Carl Hewitt")'s [Actor model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Actor_model "Actor model") anticipated the modern definition of intelligent agents. ([Hewitt, Bishop & Steiger 1973](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFHewittBishopSteiger1973)) Both John Doyle ([Doyle 1983](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFDoyle1983)) and [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky")'s popular classic _[The Society of Mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Society\_of\_Mind "The Society of Mind")_ ([Minsky 1986](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFMinsky1986)) used the word "agent". Other "modular" proposals included [Rodney Brook's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rodney_Brooks "Rodney Brooks")[subsumption architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subsumption_architecture "Subsumption architecture"), [object-oriented programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming") and others.
36.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-285 "Jump up")**This is how the most widely used textbooks of the 21st century define artificial intelligence, such as Russell and Norvig, 2021; Padgham and Winikoff, 2004; Jones, 2007; Poole and Mackworth, 2017.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202161-282)
37.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-290 "Jump up")**[Ray Kurzweil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil "Ray Kurzweil") wrote that the improvement in computer chess "is governed only by the brute force expansion of computer hardware."[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurzweil2005274-289)
38.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-291 "Jump up")**Cycle time of [Ferranti Mark 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferranti_Mark_1 "Ferranti Mark 1") was 1.2 milliseconds, which is arguably equivalent to about 833[flops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flops "Flops"). [Deep Blue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue "IBM Deep Blue") ran at 11.38[gigaflops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gigaflops "Gigaflops") (and this does not even take into account Deep Blue's special-purpose hardware for chess). _Very_ approximately, these differ by a factor of 10 7.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]
39.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-303 "Jump up")**[AlexNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlexNet "AlexNet") had 650,000 neurons and trained using [ImageNet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ImageNet "ImageNet"), augmented with reversed, cropped and tinted images. The model also used [Geoffrey Hinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton "Geoffrey Hinton")'s [dropout](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dropout_(neural_networks) "Dropout (neural networks)") technique and a [rectified linear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectifier_(neural_networks) "Rectifier (neural networks)") output function, both relatively new developments at the time.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202023%E2%80%9324-302)
40.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-305 "Jump up")**Several other laboratories had developed systems that, like AlexNet, used GPU chips and performed nearly as well as AlexNet,[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmidhuber2022-127) but AlexNet proved to be the most influential.
41.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-307 "Jump up")**See [History of AI §The problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_AI#The_problems "History of AI") above, where Hans Moravec predicted that raw power would eventually make AI "easy".
42.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-315 "Jump up")**Later research showed that there was no way for system to avoid a measurable racist bias – fixing one form of bias would necessarily introduce another.[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202067%E2%80%9370-314)
43.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-317 "Jump up")**A short summary of topics would include [privacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privacy "Privacy"), [surveillance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surveillance "Surveillance"), [copyright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copyright "Copyright"), [misinformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Misinformation "Misinformation") and [deep fakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_fakes "Deep fakes"), [filter bubbles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filter_bubbles "Filter bubbles") and [partisanship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partisanship "Partisanship"), [algorithmic bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithmic_bias "Algorithmic bias"), misleading results that go undetected without [algorithmic transparency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithmic_transparency "Algorithmic transparency"), the [right to an explanation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_to_an_explanation "Right to an explanation"), misuse of [autonomous weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autonomous_weapon "Autonomous weapon") and [technological unemployment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technological_unemployment "Technological unemployment"). See [Artificial intelligence §Ethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence#Ethics "Artificial intelligence")
44.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-321 "Jump up")**[Brian Christian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brian_Christian "Brian Christian") wrote "ProPublica's study [of COMPAS in 2015] legitimated concepts like fairness as valid topics for research"[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian202073-320)

230.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_ref-261 "Jump up")**[Expert systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_system "Expert system") failure (and the reason for it): 
    *   [Russell & Norvig 2021](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFRussellNorvig2021), p.24 (inability to handle uncertain reasoning or to learn)
    *   [McCorduck 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFMcCorduck2004), p.435 (institutional issues)
    *   [Newquist 1994](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFNewquist1994), pp.258–283 (limited deployment after development)
    *   [Crevier 1993](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFCrevier1993), pp.204–208 (the difficulty of truth maintenance, i.e., learning and updating)
    *   [Lenat & Guha 1989](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#CITEREFLenatGuha1989), Introduction (brittleness and the inability to handle extensive [qualification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qualification_problem "Qualification problem").)
